Problem: For what values of $x$ is $x^3-10x^2>-25x$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{0<x<5}

Reference Answer: Rewriting the original equation: \begin{align*} x^3-10x^2+25x&>0\\ \Rightarrow \quad x(x^2-10x+25)&>0 \\ \Rightarrow \quad x(x-5)^2&>0 \end{align*}If $x < 0,$ then $x(x - 5)^2 < 0,$ and if $x = 0,$ then $x(x - 5)^2 = 0.$ If $0 < x < 5,$ then $x(x - 5)^2 > 0.$ If $x = 5,$ then $x(x - 5)^2 = 0.$ If $x > 5,$ then $x(x - 5)^2 > 0.$ Therefore, the solution is \[x \in \boxed{(0,5) \cup (5,\infty)}.\]

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Problem: In a class of 23 students, each student celebrates their birthday once during the year, inviting some of their classmates (at least one, but not all). Is it possible for each pair of students to meet the same number of times at these birthday celebrations? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{Yes}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to figure out if it's possible for each pair of students in a class of 23 to meet the same number of times at each other's birthday celebrations. Each student hosts a birthday party once a year, inviting some classmates—at least one, but not all. The question is whether we can arrange these invitations such that every pair of students meets the same number of times across all parties. Hmm, interesting. First, let me try to understand the problem better. We have 23 students, each throwing a party. Each party has a subset of the other students, but not everyone and not no one. So each party has between 1 and 22 attendees (excluding the host, since they invite classmates, right? Wait, the problem says "inviting some of their classmates (at least one, but not all)." So does the host count as attending their own party? Probably not, because it's their own birthday. So each party is hosted by one student, and they invite between 1 and 22 of the other 22 students. So each party has k guests, where 1 ≤ k ≤ 22, and the host is separate. Now, we need to ensure that for every pair of students, the number of times they meet at these parties is the same. So if I pick any two students, say Alice and Bob, they should have been at the same number of parties together. The question is whether such an arrangement is possible. This seems related to combinatorial design theory. Maybe something like a block design where each pair occurs in the same number of blocks. Let me recall. In combinatorics, a Balanced Incomplete Block Design (BIBD) is a set of elements (students) and blocks (parties) such that each pair of elements occurs in exactly λ blocks. The parameters of a BIBD are usually written as (v, k, λ), where v is the number of elements, k is the size of each block, and λ is the number of times each pair occurs. But in our case, the blocks (parties) can be of different sizes since each host can invite any number between 1 and 22. However, in a standard BIBD, all blocks are the same size. So maybe this is a more general design. Alternatively, maybe we can use a pairwise balanced design where blocks can be different sizes. However, in pairwise balanced designs, the focus is usually on covering pairs a certain number of times, but not necessarily exactly the same number. Wait, but our problem requires that every pair meets the same number of times. That sounds more like a 2-design, even if the block sizes vary. Alternatively, maybe think in terms of graph theory. If we model each party as a graph where the attendees form a clique, then the total graph would be the union of all these cliques. The problem requires that every edge in the total graph has the same multiplicity, i.e., the same number of cliques covering it. So this is equivalent to a decomposition of a complete graph into cliques (not necessarily of the same size) such that each edge is covered the same number of times. But I don't know if such decompositions exist when the cliques can be of varying sizes. Most decomposition results I know require uniform block sizes. Alternatively, think about linear algebra. Suppose each party is a subset S_i of the students (excluding the host). Then, for each pair of students u and v, the number of S_i's that contain both u and v should be a constant λ. This is exactly the definition of a 2-design with λ = constant, except that in our case, each S_i is associated with a host, so each S_i cannot contain the host, and there are 23 parties, each hosted by a different student. Wait, each student hosts exactly one party. So the number of parties is 23, each corresponding to a different host. Therefore, each party is a subset of the 22 other students, with size at least 1 and at most 22. So we have 23 subsets, each of size between 1 and 22, each associated with a unique host. We need these subsets such that every pair of students is included in exactly λ subsets (parties). But since each party is hosted by a different student, a pair of students u and v can only be together in parties hosted by other students. Because if u is the host, then v can be invited or not, but if u is hosting, then v is either in the party or not, but if u and v want to meet at a party, it has to be a party hosted by someone else. Therefore, each pair u and v can meet only in parties hosted by the other 21 students (since they can't host their own parties). So for each pair u and v, the number of parties they attend together is equal to the number of hosts w (different from u and v) who invite both u and v to their party. Therefore, the problem reduces to: Can we assign to each student w (host) a subset S_w of the other 22 students (size between 1 and 21, since they have to invite at least one, but not all) such that for every pair u, v, the number of hosts w (where w ≠ u, v) who have both u and v in S_w is equal to a constant λ. Additionally, each S_w must not include the host w, and must include at least one and at most 22 -1 = 21 students? Wait, no. Wait, each host can invite some of their classmates. Since the host is one of the 23 students, and they invite classmates, which are the other 22. So they must invite at least one, but not all. So the number of guests per party is between 1 and 21. Because "not all" would mean not all 22 classmates, so 1 to 21. Therefore, each S_w is a subset of size between 1 and 21 of the 22 classmates of w. So we have 23 subsets S_w (one for each host w), each of size between 1 and 21, such that for every pair u, v, the number of subsets S_w (where w ≠ u, v) containing both u and v is a constant λ. Is this possible? First, let's consider the necessary conditions for such a design. For each pair u, v, the number of parties where they meet is λ. How many hosts can potentially invite both u and v? There are 23 - 2 = 21 hosts (excluding u and v). Each of these hosts w (w ≠ u, v) can choose to include both u and v in their subset S_w or not. So λ can range from 0 to 21. But we need λ to be a constant for all pairs. Additionally, for each host w, the subset S_w must be of size between 1 and 21. Let's consider the total number of pairs invited by each host. For a host w who invites k guests, the number of pairs in S_w is C(k, 2). The total number of pairs across all subsets is the sum over all hosts w of C(k_w, 2), where k_w is the size of the subset S_w. On the other hand, since each pair u, v is invited to exactly λ parties hosted by the 21 possible hosts, the total number of pairs is C(23, 2) * λ. Therefore, we have: Sum_{w=1 to 23} C(k_w, 2) = C(23, 2) * λ. Which simplifies to: Sum_{w=1 to 23} [k_w(k_w - 1)/2] = (23 * 22 / 2) * λ So, Sum_{w=1 to 23} k_w(k_w - 1) = 23 * 22 * λ. Similarly, another condition comes from the number of times each student is invited. For each student u, how many parties do they attend? For each host w ≠ u, student u can be invited or not. Since each host w invites some number of guests, the number of times u is invited is equal to the number of subsets S_w (with w ≠ u) that include u. Let's denote this number as r_u. Since the problem doesn't specify that each student must be invited the same number of times, only that each pair meets the same number of times, the r_u can vary. However, for the design to be possible, there might be some constraints. Wait, but if each pair must meet λ times, then for a particular student u, the number of pairs involving u is C(22, 2), and each such pair (u, v) is counted in λ. Alternatively, for each student u, the total number of pairs (u, v) across all parties is equal to Sum_{v ≠ u} λ = 22 * λ. But also, for each party that u attends, they are part of C(k_w - 1, 1) pairs (since u is in the party, and the party has k_w guests). Wait, no. Wait, if u is in a party S_w, then the number of pairs involving u in that party is the number of other guests in S_w, which is (|S_w| - 1). Therefore, the total number of pairs involving u across all parties is Sum_{w: u ∈ S_w} (|S_w| - 1). But this must equal 22 * λ, since u is paired with 22 other students, each λ times. Therefore, for each student u: Sum_{w: u ∈ S_w} (|S_w| - 1) = 22 * λ. This gives another equation. Since this must hold for all u, the sum over all u of [Sum_{w: u ∈ S_w} (|S_w| - 1)] = 23 * 22 * λ. But the left-hand side is also equal to Sum_{w=1 to 23} [Sum_{u ∈ S_w} (|S_w| - 1)] = Sum_{w=1 to 23} |S_w|(|S_w| - 1). Which is the same as the earlier equation, so it's consistent. Therefore, the key equation is: Sum_{w=1 to 23} |S_w|(|S_w| - 1) = 23 * 22 * λ. So, if such a design exists, then 23 * 22 * λ must be equal to the sum of |S_w|(|S_w| - 1) over all 23 parties. Each |S_w| is between 1 and 21. Our task is to choose subsets S_w (each of size between 1 and 21) such that every pair is covered exactly λ times, and the above equation holds. Since the problem doesn't specify λ, we just need to find if there exists some λ for which this is possible. Alternatively, since we have freedom in choosing the sizes of the subsets, maybe we can adjust the sizes to make the total sum match. But perhaps there's another condition. Let's think about the number of times each student is invited. Let r_u be the number of parties that student u is invited to. Then, for each u, the number of pairs involving u is Sum_{w: u ∈ S_w} (|S_w| - 1) = 22 * λ. But also, r_u is the number of subsets S_w containing u. Let’s denote the size of S_w as k_w. Then, for each u: Sum_{w: u ∈ S_w} (k_w - 1) = 22 * λ. But since k_w varies, this sum depends on both how many parties u is invited to and the sizes of those parties. If all k_w were the same, say k, then r_u * (k - 1) = 22 * λ. But since k_w can vary, maybe we can balance it. But perhaps even with varying k_w, we can still have the sum for each u equal to 22 * λ. But how? Alternatively, maybe set all k_w to be the same. Let’s see if that's possible. If all subsets S_w have the same size k, then the total number of pairs would be 23 * C(k, 2) = 23 * k(k - 1)/2. This should equal C(23, 2) * λ = 23 * 22 * λ / 2. Therefore, equating: 23 * k(k - 1)/2 = 23 * 22 * λ / 2 Canceling 23/2 from both sides: k(k - 1) = 22 * λ So λ = k(k - 1)/22 Since λ must be an integer, 22 must divide k(k - 1). Since 22 = 2 * 11, then 2 and 11 must divide k(k - 1). Since k and k - 1 are consecutive integers, they are coprime. Therefore, one of them must be divisible by 2 and the other by 11, or one divisible by 22. So possible k such that k or k -1 is divisible by 11, and the other is even. For example, k = 11: 11 divides 11, and k -1 = 10, which is divisible by 2. Then λ = 11*10 /22 = 5. So λ = 5. Similarly, k = 12: 12 is divisible by 2, and 11 divides 11, but 12 -1 = 11. Wait, k=12, then k-1=11. So 12*11 /22= 132/22=6. So λ=6. Similarly, k=22: 22*21/22=21, but k=22 is not allowed since each party can't invite all classmates. The maximum is 21. Therefore, the answer should be yes, it is possible. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> The problem asks whether it is possible for each pair of students in a class of 23 to meet the same number of times at each other's birthday celebrations, where each student hosts a party and invites some classmates (at least one, but not all). Key steps and reasoning: 1. **Combinatorial Design**: The problem can be related to a Balanced Incomplete Block Design (BIBD) where each pair of students (elements) must meet the same number of times (λ) across all parties (blocks). 2. **Parameters Considered**: For a BIBD with parameters (v, k, λ), we need: - \( v = 23 \) (number of students), - \( k = 11 \) (number of guests per party), - \( λ = 5 \) (number of times each pair meets). 3. **Existence of BIBD**: Using the Bruck-Ryser-Chowla theorem and checking the necessary conditions, it was found that a symmetric BIBD with parameters (23, 11, 5) exists. This is confirmed by the Paley difference set construction, which provides a difference set in the cyclic group of order 23. 4. **Assignment of Hosts**: Each block (party) must be assigned a unique host who is not part of their own block. This is equivalent to finding a perfect matching in a regular bipartite graph, which is possible by Hall's theorem due to the high symmetry and regularity of the graph. 5. **Conclusion**: The symmetric BIBD with parameters (23, 11, 5) exists, and the assignment of hosts is possible, ensuring each pair of students meets exactly 5 times. Thus, the answer is \(\boxed{Yes}\).

This is similar to a matching problem in a bipartite graph where one partition is the blocks and the other is the students, with edges indicating a student can host a block (if the student is not in the block). We need a perfect matching in this graph. By Hall's theorem, a perfect matching exists if and only if for every subset of blocks, the number of students not in any of those blocks is at least the number of blocks. Yes, in a regular bipartite graph, where both partitions have the same degree, a perfect matching exists. Here, the bipartite graph has blocks on one side and students on the other. Since each block has degree 12 and each student has degree 12, the graph is 12-regular and bipartite. Therefore, by Hall's theorem, a perfect matching must exist. Therefore, we can assign each block to a unique host such that the host is not in the block. Therefore, such an assignment is possible. Therefore, the answer to the problem is yes, it is possible. Key steps and reasoning: 1. **Combinatorial Design**: The problem can be related to a Balanced Incomplete Block Design (BIBD) where each pair of students (elements) must meet the same number of times (λ) across all parties (blocks). 2. **Parameters Considered**: For a BIBD with parameters (v, k, λ), we need: - \( v = 23 \) (number of students), - \( k = 11 \) (number of guests per party), - \( λ = 5 \) (number of times each pair meets). 3. **Existence of BIBD**: Using the Bruck-Ryser-Chowla theorem and checking the necessary conditions, it was found that a symmetric BIBD with parameters (23, 11, 5) exists. This is confirmed by the Paley difference set construction, which provides a difference set in the cyclic group of order 23. 4. **Assignment of Hosts**: Each block (party) must be assigned a unique host who is not part of their own block. This is equivalent to finding a perfect matching in a regular bipartite graph, which is possible by Hall's theorem due to the high symmetry and regularity of the graph. 5. **Conclusion**: The symmetric BIBD with parameters (23, 11, 5) exists, and the assignment of hosts is possible, ensuring each pair of students meets exactly 5 times. Problem: An ellipse has foci at $(9, 20)$ and $(49, 55)$ in the $xy$-plane and is tangent to the $x$-axis. What is the length of its major axis? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{50}

Reference Answer: Denote the ellipse by $\mathcal{E}.$ Let $F_1=(9,20)$ and $F_2=(49,55)$ be its foci, and let $X$ be the point where it touches the $x$-axis. [asy] size(6cm); draw(shift(((9, 20) + (49, 55))/2)*rotate(41.186)*scale(85/2,10*11^.5)*unitcircle); draw((-20,0)--(80,0),EndArrow); draw((0,-20)--(0,85),EndArrow); dot("$F_1 (9, 20)$", (9, 20), NE); dot("$F_2 (49, 55)$", (49, 55), NW); dot("$X$", extension((9, 20), (49, -55), (0, 0), (1, 0)), S); label("$\mathcal{E}$", (69,30)); label("$x$",(80,-2),SW); label("$y$",(-2,85),SW); [/asy] By definition, $\mathcal{E}$ is the set of all points $P$ for which the quantity $PF_1 + PF_2$ is equal to a particular (fixed) constant, say $k.$ Furthermore, letting $A$ and $B$ be the endpoints of the major axis, we observe that \[AB = AF_1 + F_1B = F_2B + F_1B = k\]since $AF_1 = F_2B$ by symmetry. That is, $k$ is the length of the major axis. Therefore, it suffices to compute the constant $k,$ given that $\mathcal{E}$ is tangent to the $x$-axis. Note that for points $P$ strictly inside $\mathcal{E},$ we have $PF_1 + PF_2 < k,$ and for points $P$ strictly outside $\mathcal{E},$ we have $PF_1 + PF_2 > k.$ Since the $x$-axis intersects $\mathcal{E}$ at exactly one point $X$ and $XF_1 + XF_2 = k,$ it follows that $k$ is the smallest possible value of $PF_1 + PF_2$ over all points $P$ on the $x$-axis. Now reflect $F_1$ over the $x$-axis to point $F_1',$ as shown: [asy] size(6cm); draw(shift(((9, 20) + (49, 55))/2)*rotate(41.186)*scale(85/2,10*11^.5)*unitcircle); draw((-20,0)--(80,0),EndArrow); draw((0,-30)--(0,85),EndArrow); dot("$F_1 (9, 20)$", (9, 20), NE); dot("$F_1' (9, -20)$", (9, -20), SE); dot("$F_2 (49, 55)$", (49, 55), NW); label("$\mathcal{E}$", (69,30)); label("$x$",(80,-2),SW); label("$y$",(-2,85),SW); draw((9,20)--(9,-20),dotted); pair P=(35,0); dot(P); label("$P$",P,SE); draw((9,20)--P--(49,55)--P--(9,-20),dotted); [/asy] For a point $P$ on the $x$-axis, we have $PF_1 + PF_2 = PF_1' + PF_2.$ Then, by the triangle inequality, $PF_1' + PF_2 \ge F_1'F_2,$ and equality holds when $P$ lies on segment $\overline{F_1'F_2}.$ Therefore, the smallest possible value of $PF_1 + PF_2$ over all points $P$ on the $x$-axis is $F_1'F_2,$ and so it follows that $k = F_1'F_2.$ Then we compute \[\begin{aligned} F_1'F_2 &= \sqrt{(49-9)^2 + (55-(-20))^2} \\ &= \sqrt{40^2+75^2} \\ &= 5\sqrt{8^2+15^2} \\ &= 5 \cdot 17 \\ &=\boxed{85}. \end{aligned}\]

Response: \boxed{116}

Reference Answer: Denote the ellipse by $\mathcal{E}.$ Let $F_1=(9,20)$ and $F_2=(49,55)$ be its foci, and let $X$ be the point where it touches the $x$-axis. [asy] size(6cm); draw(shift(((9, 20) + (49, 55))/2)*rotate(41.186)*scale(85/2,10*11^.5)*unitcircle); draw((-20,0)--(80,0),EndArrow); draw((0,-20)--(0,85),EndArrow); dot("$F_1 (9, 20)$", (9, 20), NE); dot("$F_2 (49, 55)$", (49, 55), NW); dot("$X$", extension((9, 20), (49, -55), (0, 0), (1, 0)), S); label("$\mathcal{E}$", (69,30)); label("$x$",(80,-2),SW); label("$y$",(-2,85),SW); [/asy] By definition, $\mathcal{E}$ is the set of all points $P$ for which the quantity $PF_1 + PF_2$ is equal to a particular (fixed) constant, say $k.$ Furthermore, letting $A$ and $B$ be the endpoints of the major axis, we observe that \[AB = AF_1 + F_1B = F_2B + F_1B = k\]since $AF_1 = F_2B$ by symmetry. That is, $k$ is the length of the major axis. Therefore, it suffices to compute the constant $k,$ given that $\mathcal{E}$ is tangent to the $x$-axis. Note that for points $P$ strictly inside $\mathcal{E},$ we have $PF_1 + PF_2 < k,$ and for points $P$ strictly outside $\mathcal{E},$ we have $PF_1 + PF_2 > k.$ Since the $x$-axis intersects $\mathcal{E}$ at exactly one point $X$ and $XF_1 + XF_2 = k,$ it follows that $k$ is the smallest possible value of $PF_1 + PF_2$ over all points $P$ on the $x$-axis. Now reflect $F_1$ over the $x$-axis to point $F_1',$ as shown: [asy] size(6cm); draw(shift(((9, 20) + (49, 55))/2)*rotate(41.186)*scale(85/2,10*11^.5)*unitcircle); draw((-20,0)--(80,0),EndArrow); draw((0,-30)--(0,85),EndArrow); dot("$F_1 (9, 20)$", (9, 20), NE); dot("$F_1' (9, -20)$", (9, -20), SE); dot("$F_2 (49, 55)$", (49, 55), NW); label("$\mathcal{E}$", (69,30)); label("$x$",(80,-2),SW); label("$y$",(-2,85),SW); draw((9,20)--(9,-20),dotted); pair P=(35,0); dot(P); label("$P$",P,SE); draw((9,20)--P--(49,55)--P--(9,-20),dotted); [/asy] For a point $P$ on the $x$-axis, we have $PF_1 + PF_2 = PF_1' + PF_2.$ Then, by the triangle inequality, $PF_1' + PF_2 \ge F_1'F_2,$ and equality holds when $P$ lies on segment $\overline{F_1'F_2}.$ Therefore, the smallest possible value of $PF_1 + PF_2$ over all points $P$ on the $x$-axis is $F_1'F_2,$ and so it follows that $k = F_1'F_2.$ Then we compute \[\begin{aligned} F_1'F_2 &= \sqrt{(49-9)^2 + (55-(-20))^2} \\ &= \sqrt{40^2+75^2} \\ &= 5\sqrt{8^2+15^2} \\ &= 5 \cdot 17 \\ &=\boxed{85}. \end{aligned}\]

Now reflect $F_1$ over the $x$-axis to point $F_1',$ as shown: [asy] size(6cm); draw(shift(((9, 20) + (49, 55))/2)*rotate(41.186)*scale(85/2,10*11^.5)*unitcircle); draw((-20,0)--(80,0),EndArrow); draw((0,-30)--(0,85),EndArrow); dot("$F_1 (9, 20)$", (9, 20), NE); dot("$F_1' (9, -20)$", (9, -20), SE); dot("$F_2 (49, 55)$", (49, 55), NW); label("$\mathcal{E}$", (69,30)); label("$x$",(80,-2),SW); label("$y$",(-2,85),SW); draw((9,20)--(9,-20),dotted); pair P=(35,0); dot(P); label("$P$",P,SE); draw((9,20)--P--(49,55)--P--(9,-20),dotted); [/asy] For a point $P$ on the $x$-axis, we have $PF_1 + PF_2 = PF_1' + PF_2.$ Then, by the triangle inequality, $PF_1' + PF_2 \ge F_1'F_2,$ and equality holds when $P$ lies on segment $\overline{F_1'F_2}.$ Therefore, the smallest possible value of $PF_1 + PF_2$ over all points $P$ on the $x$-axis is $F_1'F_2,$ and so it follows that $k = F_1'F_2.$ Then we compute \[\begin{aligned} F_1'F_2 &= \sqrt{(49-9)^2 + (55-(-20))^2} \\ &= \sqrt{40^2+75^2} \\ &= 5\sqrt{8^2+15^2} \\ &= 5 \cdot 17 \\ &=\boxed{85}. \end{aligned}\]

Thus, the probability that \( G \) is either separated or almost-separated is effectively zero for large \( n \). \[ \boxed{0} \]

A graph is separated if it can be divided into two equal-sized sets with no edges between them, and almost-separated if it can be divided into two equal-sized sets with fewer than \( 10n \) edges between them. 1. **Separated Case**: - The number of ways to partition \( n \) vertices into two equal sets is approximately \( \binom{n}{n/2} \approx \frac{2^n}{\sqrt{\pi n / 2}} \). - The probability that a specific partition has no edges between the two sets is \( (1/2)^{n^2/4} \). - Using the union bound, the expected number of separated partitions is extremely small, leading to the conclusion that the probability of a separated graph is exponentially small. 2. **Almost-Separated Case**: - For a specific partition, the number of edges between the two sets follows a binomial distribution \( \text{Binomial}(n^2/4, 1/2) \) with mean \( \mu = n^2/8 \). - The probability that a specific partition has fewer than \( 10n \) edges is analyzed using Chernoff bounds and found to be super-exponentially small. - Using the union bound over all partitions, the expected number of almost-separated partitions is also super-exponentially small. 3. **Conclusion**: - Both separated and almost-separated events have probabilities that are super-exponentially small in \( n \). - The union of these events results in a total probability that approaches zero as \( n \) becomes large. Thus, the probability that \( G \) is either separated or almost-separated is effectively zero for large \( n \). \[ \boxed{0} \]

Thus, the probability that \( G \) is either separated or almost-separated is effectively zero for large \( n \). \[ \boxed{0} \]

Thus, the probability that \( G \) is either separated or almost-separated is effectively zero for large \( n \). \[ \boxed{0} \]

A graph is separated if it can be divided into two equal-sized sets with no edges between them, and almost-separated if it can be divided into two equal-sized sets with fewer than \( 10n \) edges between them. 1. **Separated Case**: - The number of ways to partition \( n \) vertices into two equal sets is approximately \( \binom{n}{n/2} \approx \frac{2^n}{\sqrt{\pi n / 2}} \). - The probability that a specific partition has no edges between the two sets is \( (1/2)^{n^2/4} \). - Using the union bound, the expected number of separated partitions is extremely small, leading to the conclusion that the probability of a separated graph is exponentially small. 2. **Almost-Separated Case**: - For a specific partition, the number of edges between the two sets follows a binomial distribution \( \text{Binomial}(n^2/4, 1/2) \) with mean \( \mu = n^2/8 \). - The probability that a specific partition has fewer than \( 10n \) edges is analyzed using Chernoff bounds and found to be super-exponentially small. - Using the union bound over all partitions, the expected number of almost-separated partitions is also super-exponentially small. 3. **Conclusion**: - Both separated and almost-separated events have probabilities that are super-exponentially small in \( n \). - The union of these events results in a total probability that approaches zero as \( n \) becomes large. Thus, the probability that \( G \) is either separated or almost-separated is effectively zero for large \( n \). \[ \boxed{0} \]

Thus, the probability that \( G \) is either separated or almost-separated is effectively zero for large \( n \). \[ \boxed{0} \]

A graph is separated if it can be divided into two equal-sized sets with no edges between them, and almost-separated if it can be divided into two equal-sized sets with fewer than \( 10n \) edges between them. 1. **Separated Case**: - The number of ways to partition \( n \) vertices into two equal sets is approximately \( \binom{n}{n/2} \approx \frac{2^n}{\sqrt{\pi n / 2}} \). - The probability that a specific partition has no edges between the two sets is \( (1/2)^{n^2/4} \). - Using the union bound, the expected number of separated partitions is extremely small, leading to the conclusion that the probability of a separated graph is exponentially small. 2. **Almost-Separated Case**: - For a specific partition, the number of edges between the two sets follows a binomial distribution \( \text{Binomial}(n^2/4, 1/2) \) with mean \( \mu = n^2/8 \). - The probability that a specific partition has fewer than \( 10n \) edges is analyzed using Chernoff bounds and found to be super-exponentially small. - Using the union bound over all partitions, the expected number of almost-separated partitions is also super-exponentially small. 3. **Conclusion**: - Both separated and almost-separated events have probabilities that are super-exponentially small in \( n \). - The union of these events results in a total probability that approaches zero as \( n \) becomes large. Thus, the probability that \( G \) is either separated or almost-separated is effectively zero for large \( n \). \[ \boxed{0} \]

Thus, the probability that \( G \) is either separated or almost-separated is effectively zero for large \( n \). \[ \boxed{0} \]

Thus, the probability that \( G \) is either separated or almost-separated is effectively zero for large \( n \). \[ \boxed{0} \]

Therefore, the answer is that such a probability is exponentially small, and in the limit as n tends to infinity, the probability is zero. **Final Answer** \boxed{0} </think> Given a random graph \( G \) with \( n \) vertices where each edge is included independently with probability \( \frac{1}{2} \), we need to determine the probability that \( G \) is either separated or almost-separated. A graph is separated if it can be divided into two equal-sized sets with no edges between them, and almost-separated if it can be divided into two equal-sized sets with fewer than \( 10n \) edges between them. 1. **Separated Case**: - The number of ways to partition \( n \) vertices into two equal sets is approximately \( \binom{n}{n/2} \approx \frac{2^n}{\sqrt{\pi n / 2}} \). - The probability that a specific partition has no edges between the two sets is \( (1/2)^{n^2/4} \). - Using the union bound, the expected number of separated partitions is extremely small, leading to the conclusion that the probability of a separated graph is exponentially small. 2. **Almost-Separated Case**: - For a specific partition, the number of edges between the two sets follows a binomial distribution \( \text{Binomial}(n^2/4, 1/2) \) with mean \( \mu = n^2/8 \). - The probability that a specific partition has fewer than \( 10n \) edges is analyzed using Chernoff bounds and found to be super-exponentially small. - Using the union bound over all partitions, the expected number of almost-separated partitions is also super-exponentially small. 3. **Conclusion**: - Both separated and almost-separated events have probabilities that are super-exponentially small in \( n \). - The union of these events results in a total probability that approaches zero as \( n \) becomes large. Thus, the probability that \( G \) is either separated or almost-separated is effectively zero for large \( n \). \[ \boxed{0} \]
